B00005Y7AW|5.0|Poses is the second album by Rufus Wainwright. Written mostly while he was staying in New York's famous Chelsea Hotel, the album took almost two years to record and was released in 2001, three years after his debut. 11 years later many consider it his best album. It's certainly a unique album in his discography, with a clearly defined and consistent sound, as well as a tone and aura that hasn't been seen in his music before or since. Besides the largely piano and voice album [[ASIN:B0038JH3AU All Days Are Nights: Songs For Lulu]], Poses is his most simplistic, least extravagant record and the album that should have made him a household name. It's a sad truth that not too many people know who Rufus Wainwright is, but when I meet someone who does I've noticed a trend of them knowing the song Cigarettes and Chocolate Milk. It's arguably one of his most identifiable songs; a beautiful track that certainly had the potential to be a commercial hit. Hearing this song by accident in the trailer for a Frnech film a couple years ago is what exposed me to his music. The song is so great that it's on the album twice. The reprise has some added percussion and seems like an unnecessary addition, but I don't mind its inclusion.<br /><br />If I were to describe Poses in one word, it would be hypnotic. This is an album with atmosphere that casts a spell over the listener. The first 8 songs alone make it a masterwork. The phenomenal Greek Song is arguably one of Rufus' best songs, with a melody that projects quiet emotion and true feeling. The title track is inarguably one of his best songs and I can't help but think it may be one of the most beautiful songs ever recorded. With his soft, achingly beautiful voice at the forefront; it's a painfully honest document of what was going on in his life at the time. And who else but Rufus Wainwright could pull off a line like \\"I did go from wanting to be someone, now I'm drunk and wearing flip-flops on Fifth Avenue\\" so flawlessly?<br /><br />The dreamy, hypnotic, and funky Shadows is followed by California, another song that had the potential to bring him commercial success. It may be the catchiest song on the record. The Tower of Learning is a lovely, soaring track while the catchy, piano-driven Grey Gardens has lyrics that cleverly intertwine themes and characters from the film of the same name and the book/film Death in Venice. Finally, Rebel Prince is a soft, mostly acoustic song with whispered vocals (sung in French at one point) that just seethes beauty.<br /><br />The latter half admittedly isn't as strong as what came before it, but it's still powerful. The Consort has some beauty to it, but has never impressed me as much as the other tracks. It's one of those songs that either languishes in the background or prompts you to skip it. One Man Guy is a song originally written and recorded by his father Loudon Wainwright III. Given Rufus' sexuality, he adds irony, humor, and another layer of depth to this great tune that rivals his father's version.<br /><br />Evil Angel is the most atmospheric song on the record, with the closest resemblance to the extravagant compositions you'll find on his later records. Meanwhile, In a Graveyard is simply a short, minimalist track of just piano and voice that may be his highest lyrical achievement on the record. The albm ends with the bonus track Across the Universe. There's a level of heresy that I don't care to broach when comparing a Beatles song to the subsequent cover, but let me assure you...This is a solid rendition that is complemented perfectly by Wainwright's beautiful voice.<br /><br />Rufus has never been secretive about the fact that he began recording Poses to be a radio-friendly record. Rather than being a symbol of him selling out to the mainstream, it merely solidified his place as one of the most talented singer/songwriters alive. It's his most straight-forward record, with emphasis on his singing and songwriting ability rather than his penchant for overblown production and extravagant compositions. That's not a criticism of his later records, just a comment about this one. I love every record he has done, so to pick a singular favorite would be impossible for me.<br /><br />You may have noticed by how many times I've used the words \\"lovely\\" and \\"beautiful\\" that I adore Rufus Wainwright and his music. This review comes from a deeply-rooted bias, but it's hard to deny biased or not what a great record this is. Poses is his most subtle and (until his latest record [[ASIN:B006ZZANG0 Out of the Game]]) his most accessible album, but it's really terrific and a good starting point to allow yourself to bask in the beauty and brilliance of his music. If you've heard even one song you like by the man, this shouldn't disappoint you.

B004QOB8SO|5.0|I like this series a lot more than those Robert Downy, Jr. movies.  It does a better job striking a balance between being true to the original novels and yet making it accessible to a modern audience.<br /><br />For instance, both the RDJ movies and the new TV show are more face-paced and action-oriented than the original novels.  But the action in the movies is over-the-top and cartoonish.  The action in the TV show is more realistic.  (It's by no means entirely realistic and definitely has some fantastical elements.  It's certainly not like you're not watching \\"The Wire\\".  But relative to the movies, it's nearly not as cartoonish.)<br /><br />Also, in both the RDJ movies and the TV show the character of Holmes is a super genius who's egotistical and arrogant.  But the cockiness of RDJ's Holmes is somewhat whimsical and tongue-in-cheek.  It's like he's trying to be cool.  He's the class clown who is popular because of, not in spite of, his being an jerk.  The TV show Holmes is more of a straight-up jerk, a bit like the character of Dr. House in the TV show \\"House\\".<br /><br />The RDJ Holmes seems to be aware of social conventions, etiquette and courtesies, he just thinks they're stupid. He intentionally breaks them in protest or to get a reaction.  However, the TV show Holmes is mostly oblivious to social norms.  He violates them out of ignorance.  There's an Aspergian quality to him, like the character of Sheldon in The Big Bang Theory.<br /><br />Overall I'd say that, despite being set in modern London, the tone of the show is more on par with the books.<br /><br />Finally, the TV version of Moriarty is very original, fiendish, creepy and entertaining.  He reminds me of the Joker from \\"The Dark Knight\\".

B007WV05GY|3.0|The basic problem of The Grey is the difference between what the filmmakers think they had and what the marketing department wanted to sell. This is promoted as a dramatic survival action movie in the trailers and even Liam Neeson's talk-show circuit tour pushed the same line - a plane crashes and a bunch of guys have to survive things that want to eat them. Kind of 'Alive' meets Jurassic Park. With Ra's al Ghul.<br /><br />Unfortunately, it's this difference that's going to disappoint most of the audience. The film starts with Liam's melancholic voice-over (which isn't a good sign) and a fairly dim setup of oil-riggers beating the ever-loving out of each other. We then see Liam shooting a wolf, lamenting the loss of his wife and then putting a rifle in his mouth and contemplating sparing us from another 2 hours of this. And it gets more depressing from there when the trip home ends abruptly in a plane crash.<br /><br />It's at this point that the film's indecision about what it is becomes a real problem. As an action or survival film, Liam guides his band of survivors in the most irresponsible way possible, making mistake after mistake and fundamentally dooming them all to becoming wolf entree. There are some basic errors, such as a stack of shotgun shells packed with his rifle, and anybody who's familiar with wolf behavior is going to be really unhappy at their depiction in this film. Anyone from Alaska is going to be even more unhappy at the depiction of swimming in rapids too.<br /><br />Also as an action film, the wolves have a supernatural capability to outsmart the survivors and behave more like Spielberg's raptors than any real life wolf. At one point they jump a ravine and the wolfs are waiting with GPS precision. They pick off our band of survivors one at a time, leaving us with intermittent testosterone-fueled in-fighting to pass as drama. If only Liam hadn't left the crash site, where rescue teams would already have arrived with burgers and cocoa.<br /><br />But here's the problem - it's not an action film. It's not a survival film either. It's a psychological drama about facing death where the wolves act as furry metaphors for the grim reaper. From the very first scene to the last, Liam Neeson (who's very good as always) emphasizes this theme and it reverberates over and over. And that's where the film could have been quite good and a welcome break from the survival genre but unfortunately it's too late by the time it becomes clear that the wolves' uncanny supernatural sense has a very good explanation.<br /><br />My guess is that the everyone expecting an action drama is going to be very unhappy, along with animal rights, PETA, Alaskans and oil-riggers. It's a real shame they didn't recut the trailer before the release.br />But here's the problem - it's not an action film. It's not a survival film either. B003Y5HWMW|4.0|Spoilers to follow.<br /><br />I don't hate this series, but I don't particularly like it, either. If HBO cancels it right now, I won't be upset. If it runs for another 5 seasons, I'll watch. After struggling with these blu ray discs over the weekend, at this point I'm just in it for the LULZ. You have to laugh at the menu design, otherwise you'll throw the box set against the wall and cry.<br /><br />Short Version:<br />There was a flock of dragons, and a very badly designed blu ray menu. That's about it. If you'd like the details, read on.<br /><br />The Blu Ray Discs:<br />I don't know what the DVDs are like, but the blu rays make me mad as a hatter. I have seen complaints from many people who can't get them to load at all. I'm using a Sony BDP-S350 1080p Blu-ray Disc Player that I bought in 2009, and updated my firmware before I even bothered to insert a disc. They do take a very long time to load, but once they were loaded I didn't have problems beyond the confusing menus. Still, be prepared for potential problems.<br /><br />Frustration comes in with menus and having to constantly switch discs for bonus content. Whoever designed the menus, content and storage on these things should be trapped on a desert island with Joffrey Baratheon with no sunscreen. If you're in it just for the show, you should be fine. If you want to watch the extras, just know in advance that you are going to be as confused and frustrated as you are with the Cast of Thousands. Add to that about two dozen copyright notices that appear randomly in different languages after certain featurettes, and you may want to give the discs the same fate as Ned Stark.<br /><br />Some of the extras are on the discs where they say they are, and some of them are not. Let's say you want to watch episode one with commentary. It's on disc one with episode one. Same with episode two. Go to episode three, and it tells you to switch discs to watch episode three commentary. It does this throughout the set, sometimes sending you to the next disc, and sometimes sending you to the previous disc. After a certain point, you've forgotten where you were and you have to stop and figure out what episode you were on, and you wonder whether it's worth getting up 8 times just to get the extras.<br /><br />I was hoping the commentary would give me some more insight into what was going on in the show, but this sadly didn't pan out. The first episode with David Benioff and Dan Weiss does exactly what I wanted. There's another one with one of the directors giving good information, but the rest of the commentary really didn't contribute much. Or maybe I was just too frustrated with the menu to care at that point. Lena Heady pointed out something about Cersei's relationship with Sansa Stark that had not occurred to me, but that's the only useful thing I got out of it.<br /><br />A lot of the featurettes have been available on You Tube on HBO's own channel since the beginning of the series. Still, there are ones I have never seen and found quite interesting, like the making of the opening of the show, so you may find some great content here--if you can find the right disc. So the extra features are quite the mixed bag.<br /><br />Season One:<br />This is a pretty good series, but it's got problems. It's supposed to be high fantasy, but it comes across as more alternate history. I think the rule for fantasy writers these days is to use as little magic as possible, but this series has been stripped of too much. The only hint of magic or otherworldliness comes at the end of the series with the appearance of the dragons. To make up for the lack of magic, they throw in a bunch of \\"gritty\\" stuff to make it more \\"real,\\" and I do mean they throw in everything--rape, incest, prostitution, graphic violence, and a potpourri of other disturbing images and events.<br /><br />Game of Thrones has way too much expository dialogue. It gets bogged down with characters talking about stuff that happened off screen years ago. Most of the time this is done to establish character, even though we already know what the character is like. We may even already know the story and it ends up being a repeat. They just keep talking and talking and will not shut up. Just when you think something is going to happen, someone launches into a long speech about some past traumatic event. Even Tyrion does this, and it's agonizingly long.<br /><br />They even do this during the quintessential HBO sex scenes, which themselves get way out of hand. I know this is HBO, and it's going to be graphic, but there's a point where it turns into bad soft porn and contributes absolutely nothing to story, character or anything else. I know this is why some people tune in, I know some of it is expected, I'm not a prude... it's just that the show takes it beyond the usual levels of absurdity.<br /><br />Then, there are loads and loads of characters--mostly because they keep getting killed off. This is one of those shows where anyone can die. What makes it irritating is that this often comes shortly after we've been told the character's life history and we actually start to care. A lot of times the information turns out to be unimportant, and you wish they had spent the time showing something else... anything else.<br /><br />You literally need to take notes to keep up with what's going on with so many plots and points of view. It's hard to remember all the names and relations. For example, The Lord Commander of the Night's Watch is father to Jorah Mormont. Jorah is the Wikipedia of Westeros who runs around with the Targaryen/Dothraki bunch in the Free Cities, doing a LOT of exposition along the way.<br /><br />Jorah was the one sending information about the Targaryens to the king, which is why he got a royal pardon for selling slaves. He is the one who disgraced the commander, and that's why the commander gave his heirloom sword to Jon Snow. It's hard to follow that in the series because there is so much other stuff going on. You have to watch it 2 or 3 times to get everything out of it. I had to go looking around fan web sites to figure out what was happening sometimes. The show is full of situations like this.<br /><br />In turn, the series progresses very slowly until Ned Stark's pivotal execution scene. The plot moves at the speed of smell until that point, then suddenly you've got battles, kings, hostages and negotiations all over the place. It's just as confusing at the faster pace, if not more so.<br /><br />Ultimately, the characters are the reason I stuck with the series. Peter Dinklage as the dwarf Tyrion Lannister is priceless as the highly intelligent snark knight. He's the only comedy relief in an otherwise exceptionally miserable world. At times I am just waiting for Tyrion to appear on screen and the other stuff is just time filler.<br /><br />Harry Lloyd as Viserys Targaryen was gone too soon, but what a spectacular exit! Fortunately Jack Gleeson as Joffrey Baratheon has stepped in as an entertaining character everyone can love to hate. Neither seems to have any redeeming qualities. I prefer my villains to have some bit of grey or good part to them, and I wish they would show more of Joffrey's human side.<br /><br />Sophie Turner as Sansa Stark is also one of my favorites, but most fans tend to not like her. I think it comes from Sansa's genre blindness and naivety. This changes, and I think it is interesting to see how someone who thinks the world works like fairy tales adjusts to a world as grim as this.<br /><br />I'm rating this series and blu ray set as four stars because I think most people will enjoy the main content. I'm not a huge fan, but I think almost everyone else would do well to give it a try. With so many characters and plots, most people are bound to find some part of the series they will want to follow. You're just going to have a difficult time of it if you want to enjoy the extra features.en years ago. I wanted to review it, however, as an experience for playing with young children.<br /><br />Summary - 5/5<br /><br />I have 2 kids, one 4 one 6.5. I would not recommend it as a first game, but once your kids have some basic ideas of gameplay, its cooperative nature offers a perfect blend of progressively independent action and bonding.<br /><br />Theme and appeal - 5/5<br /><br />The theme and appeal of the game is obvious. By the age of 3 or 4 most children will have been bombarded with a variety of fantasy narratives, and enjoy them. Spooky dungeons. Scary dragons. Heroic knights. While some parents may be concerned at the idea of crawling through caves and killing monsters, my personal view is that this comes very naturally to kids these days, and is in fact quite empowering - see a monster, whammo! blast it with a magic wand.<br /><br />This game creates a very tactile upfront experience of the things they have seen on TV or in story books. The little miniatures are very appealing, both to my 3 (almost 4) year old girl, and to my 6.5 year old son.<br /><br />Is it kid proof - 3/5<br /><br />This game is sturdy and durable. You will, however, need to keep a close eye on all the bits, which can be easily lost if not kept in themed baggies, and without very clear 'putting away when used' rules.<br /><br />The miniatures can probably take some abuse, but also need some care as there are bits that could be snapped off. You will also need to be disciplined with the cards. When my son gets excited, he waves his arms and is sometimes tempted to squish them inside his hands.<br /><br />Assuming you play on the floor (which I do) you may also need to make clear no walking or rolling over the board rules...<br /><br />Can a 6/7 year old contribute meaningfully, and feel fulfilled? - 4/5<br /><br />Most certainly, but with guidance. Even a three year old can choose which corner of a new tile to explore, and count out spaces (although it helps if they have learned space counting in other games first). A 6/7 year old can choose which powers they want to use, although may need some tactical/strategic guidance.<br /><br />I found the game offered plenty to make my son feel very fulfilled playing the game. We died in our first adventure, but despite this setback he was delighted to recall some of his more heroic moments, and how if we had only done this or that we might just have made it.<br /><br />Can Dad enjoy it while playing with the kids? - 5/5<br /><br />Certainly he can. One way to view it is as solo plus, in which you still may have to make a majority of the strategic decisions - which (despite some complaints about the game being too easy) are not always simple - but you also get the pleasure in sharing the experience with others.<br /><br />You can also use the game to practice your story telling skills to a sometimes quite demanding audience!<br /><br />Is it educational? - 5/5<br /><br />OK, let's be clear up front that this is not going to enhance your child's understanding of world geography, or the way plants grow. It is fantasy.<br /><br />But first up, I consider fantasy to be an important part of human culture, and one of the shared experiences that bind society together.<br /><br />Beyond that, the game teaches social play. Teamwork. Tactical and strategic decision making. Logic. (If-then, if not-then something else). Basic AI. Planning. It encourages reading, and thinking about maths and probability. It teaches rule systems, and how to best use them to your advantage. What's not to love about this, especially in an era of electronic solitude?<br /><br />Is it easy to learn/teach? - 4/5<br /><br />Again, this puts a certain amount of onus on the parent. I highly doubt your 6.5 year old willbe able to read and understand the rules without guidance.<br /><br />I made the mistake of trying a first game without understanding everything myself, and my poor son was stuck watching me sweat my way through the rules (as can be seen from another posting I made). It might be a good idea to do a solo run first.<br /><br />But once you get the basic concepts (how monsters move!), it comes quite easily. Yes, you do need to explain how the powers work. How the exploration works. How the monsters work. It is a lot to absorb at first.<br /><br />But if your kid is interested, they will pay attention, and it can be revealed progressively through gameplay.<br /><br />I am also amazed how much space a 6/7 year old has in his/her brain to absorb new rules... more than once I've opened an old card game and asked - hmm, how many do we deal to start? - only to have my son answer for me...<br /><br />Does it play out reasonably quickly? - 4/5<br /><br />I am assuming, with this score, that you know that you are in for a substantial game. i.e. This is not a ten minute frisson. The question is whether it can keep a kid's attention or not.<br /><br />In my first game, I found myself wishing it would take a little less time. Not a lot less, but right at the end - when it went past bedtime, and was starting to drag a little.<br /><br />That said, the denouement came roughly about the time I expected, and my son's attention was pretty gripped (allowing for a couple of short breaks). And there would be no harm in tweaking the game a bit... one or two fewer tiles, perhaps, before the final boss, could be one approach.<br /><br />Is it fun? - 5/5<br /><br />Yes! Its theme, fairly simple gameplay, and balance of achievement and challenge works well for kids. Everyone loves receiving a treasure. Everyone hates getting a nasty encounter.<br /><br />Great game. And given it is a system more than a game, with a lot of variety, I hope to be playing this for quite some time.

B001KVZ6HK|4.0|Rating:<br />+4 The dust still hasn't settled on the excitement of the release of The Avengers.  Joss Whedon was handed over the combined stories of four of Marvel's most iconic characters (and a few others tossed in for good measure) and asked to create a film (co-written with Zak Penn) that brought all of them together on a scale that has, at least up until this point, never been achieved.  For the most part, Whedon and company do a pretty darned good job of things.<br /><br />I've seen the film three times so far and I might actually go check it out one more time before it leaves theaters.  I saw it in 2D the first and last time and in 3D the second time.  While the 3D looks decent enough, it's only necessary for a couple of \\"epic\\" looking moments in the film.  Otherwise, the 3D adds nothing to the story.....not that it needs to add anything.<br /><br />The story itself moves at a pretty fast pace and is an excellent collection of action and plot.  While Whedon could have easily taken a ride on the Michael Bay train of excessive 'splosions and computer-generated \\"awesome,\\" he instead scattered the action throughout a very solid plot that builds on the unique personalities of the four main players (Captain America, Thor, Iron Man, and Hulk) and how they eventually come together to take on Thor's lil' adopted brother, Loki, in a war to save the planet.<br /><br />The story begins with Loki coming to Earth to acquire the Tesseract (which you are familiar with if you've seen some of the other films leading up to this, specifically Thor and Captain America).  Once it is in his control, he plans to use an army (I'll remain a bit vague here for the two people who haven't seen this film) to take over Earth and then any other world he sees fit to conquer after that.  In his way, however, is Nick Fury and S.H.I.E.L.D.  Fury sets out to gather three of Earth's mightiest heroes.  He sends Agent Coulson to find Tony Stark (Iron Man), Agent Romanoff/Black Widow to fetch Dr. Banner (Hulk), and he calls upon Captain America himself.  A fourth hero, Thor, is considered a wild card and there is no way to know if he'll even show up to help battle his little brother.<br /><br />When Loki shows up, he \\"compromises\\" Agent Barton/Hawkeye and Dr. Selvig, making them his servants.  He takes the Tesseract and sets out on his plan of world domination.<br /><br />Can Fury convince Cap, Iron Man, and Hulk to join forces against Loki?  Will Thor show up?  Can Scarlett Johansson look any hotter in this film?  You'll have to see The Avengers to find out.<br /><br />The cast is brilliant from top to bottom.  I was always fond of Tom Hiddleston's performance as Loki in Thor, and he amps it up just a bit for this film.  He's a bit more psychotic and a lot more egotistical in this go round.  Chris Evans IS Captain America.  I believed every single word that came out of his mouth as he not only took the point during the fight, but let his true blue all American ways come through.  He was a man lost in time who injected just the right amount of old fashioned American patriotism to save the day.  Robert Downey Jr. could have played the role of Iron Man in his sleep in this third outing as Tony Stark, but instead he played the funny man to Evans' straight man in this film.  All of the characters had their funny moments, but Downey seemed to be written into the film primarily for humor.  Don't get me wrong, Iron Man has plenty of epic moments in this film, but most of the laughs come from him, except for the big green guy......Hulk.  Mark Ruffalo stepped into the much maligned role of Bruce Banner, and made it his own.  He's meek, desperate to remain calm, and always warning others not to cross him.  He and Stark have a few funny moments together, but let's be honest here, we all came to see the Hulk do what he does best, which is SMASH, and he does just that to perfection once he's unleashed in the film.  Not only does Hulk have some of the most brutal moments inthis film, he has two of the funniest ones as well.  I won't spoil them for you here, but don't be surprised when you laugh out loud during a tense confrontation between the Hulk and another character.  Chris Hemsworth, much like Evans, was made for the role of Thor.  He has some of the top action moments in this film, but also handles the emotional struggle of fighting against or saving his brother quite well.<br /><br />Samuel Jackson does a solid job as Nick Fury, although his character is delegated more to a background role as he does most of his fighting against S.H.I.E.L.D. itself.  Cobie Smulders, while nice on the eyes, was pretty much vanilla as Agent Maria Hill.  Scarlett Johansson was very good as Black Widow and handled her action sequences quite well.  Jeremy Renner did a good job in the role of Clint Barton/Hawkeye.  His role was a bit limited, but he owns every minute he's on the screen.<br /><br />The real gem in this film was Clark Gregg as Agent Coulson.  His giddy fanboy reaction to Captain America was classic, and despite being a relatively small player in all of the other films, he takes center stage this time around.<br /><br />The action was very good.  It was also very loud and very fast.  Whedon loved to zoom in a lot, which led up to a number of blurry fight sequences, especially involving Hulk, Thor, and Iron Man.  He made sure that there were plenty of iconic scenes during the film, one of the best involving Captain America and Thor.  He also did a fair job of pacing with the film.<br /><br />The only knock that I really have with this movie is that some of the editing was choppy.  One particularly epic sequence involving three heroes ended with a thud, and took me out of the film for just a split second.  There were also a few scenes involving Loki's army that reminded me of the old television series V from the 1980's, and amped up the cheese a tad bit.<br /><br />Still, the fanboy in me wants to give this film five stars for the simple fact that it was pulled off.  It was fun, epic, and brilliant.  However, the amateur critic within begs me to take off one star for the fact that the editing did really bother me a couple of times.<br /><br />So is it worth multiple viewings?  Considering the fact that it's the only film I've ever paid full price to see more than once at the theater, yes, it's definitely worth multiple viewings.  I plan to own it on DVD when it comes out (September 25 is the announced date).  It's popcorn fodder with a plot, and that's a rare treat, especially during the \\"blockbuster\\" summer season for Hollywood.<br /><br />Go see this film.  Go see this film. 0307594165|5.0|I thoroughly enjoyed Toni Morrison's latest novel (novella?) \\"Home\\" and the highest praise I can give it is a twist on \\"praise\\" she's been given for some of her other novels. That is, that \\"Home\\" is NOT reminiscent of Faulkner. That's praise in my opinion. (I know, I know, lots of people love him. I don't)<br /><br />Morrison has been called Faulknerian in the past because her prose style has been often disjoint and cryptic. Many times it takes a few paragraphs or pages to figure out where you are in her novels. That is what makes me dislike Faulkner (try the first page of \\"The Sound and the Fury\\") but I accepted it in Morrison because she was better at it; eventually you found your place and her characters were worth the try.<br /><br />\\"Home\\" is a pretty straight-forward narrative with the addition of a wonderful little conceit where the main character, Frank, addresses the author directly. The book also contains a collection of vignettes about the secondary characters (Lily, Leonor) whereby Morrison shows us more about a character in a few pages than many authors can in a whole book. Morrison is a Nobel Laureate and Pulitzer Prize winner for a reason.<br /><br />The plot: Frank, with Korean war PTSD, is on a mission to save his sister, Cee - from what I don't want to spoil, but trust me, it's bad. It's a simple but elegant plot and there is a confession that both twists things up and clears things up, toward the end. It's a fast read but a thoroughly enjoyable one.
